Source: DiskANN and the Vamana Paradigm: Architecting Out-of-Core Graph-Based Vector Indexing at Billion-Point Scale
Type: Pasted Text
DiskANN and the Vamana Paradigm: Architecting Out-of-Core Graph-Based Vector Indexing at Billion-Point ScaleLarge-Scale, Out-of-Core Vector Storage FoundationsModern machine learning architectures, particularly those driving dense information retrieval, computer vision, and retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) pipelines, rely on high-dimensional vector representations to model semantic similarity.[1, 2, 3, 4] To search these spaces efficiently, systems must resolve the k-nearest neighbor search problem under strict latency and computational constraints.[1, 5, 6] Because exact nearest neighbor retrieval degenerates to a linear scan of the entire dataset in high dimensions—a phenomenon known as the curse of dimensionality—database engines must employ approximate nearest neighbor search (ANNS).[1, 7] The core objective of approximate retrieval is to maximize accuracy, typically measured in terms of 1-recall@1, while keeping query latency within single-digit milliseconds.[1, 7, 8]Before the development of out-of-core indexing architectures, high-performance approximate retrieval was split between two primary indexing paradigms, both of which face scaling constraints when handling large datasets.[1, 5] The first paradigm is built around inverted index indexing combined with vector compression techniques, most notably inverted file indexing with product quantization (IVF-PQ).[1, 5, 9] These methods cluster the vector space into numerous partitions and search only those partitions closest to the query, using lossily compressed representations stored in main memory.[1, 5, 7, 8] While these compressed setups are memory-efficient—requiring less than 64 gigabytes of random-access memory (RAM) to host indices for a billion points—their reliance on lossy representations causes a significant decline in retrieval accuracy.[1, 7, 8, 9] At billion-point scales, compressed indices typically plateau at around 50% 1-recall@1, which is unacceptable for many production applications.[1, 7, 8]The second paradigm relies on in-memory graph-based indexing structures, such as hierarchical navigable small world (HNSW) graphs and navigable small world variants.[7, 10, 11, 12] These algorithms construct sparse spatial graphs over uncompressed vector representations to navigate the space via greedy traversal.[1, 5, 13] Although in-memory graphs deliver superior retrieval latency and recall, they require the entire graph index and raw uncompressed vectors to reside in main memory.[7, 10, 14] As a result, scaling to billion-point datasets requires massive, multi-node distributed sharding architectures.[1, 7, 8] For example, a standard 100-million-point index composed of 128-dimensional floating-point vectors requires approximately 75 gigabytes of main memory, meaning a billion-point index requires hundreds of gigabytes of expensive memory distributed across several physical machines.[1, 5, 7, 8]DiskANN resolves this trade-off by establishing that commodity solid-state drives (SSDs) can support high-performance vector search.[1, 7, 15] By utilizing a specialized graph algorithm named Vamana and an optimized systems architecture, DiskANN places both the uncompressed high-precision vectors and the structural routing graph onto flash storage while maintaining a highly compressed coordinate representation in main memory.[1, 7, 8, 9] This hybrid architecture provides high recall alongside low query latency on a single physical workstation, delivering a massive density increase of points indexed per node compared to purely in-memory graph indexes.[7, 8, 10]
The Vamana Graph AlgorithmRandom Graph Initialization and Medoid IdentificationThe core indexing structure of DiskANN is based on the Vamana graph algorithm, which is optimized for greedy routing and low structural diameters.[1, 7, 11] The Vamana construction pipeline begins with an initialization phase that establishes the initial topological layout.[20, 21, 22] Rather than inserting nodes incrementally into an empty structure, the algorithm initializes the index as a random R-regular directed graph, where each data point is represented as a vertex with exactly R randomly assigned out-neighbors.[1, 5, 18, 20]Concurrently, the system computes the global medoid of the dataset.[1, 18, 23] The medoid s is defined as the vector point that minimizes the average Euclidean distance to all other points in the vector space P [1, 18, 23]:s = \arg\min_{p \in P} \frac{1}{|P|} \sum_{v \in P} d(p, v)This medoid s serves as the global entry point for all graph traversal operations during both index construction and query-time search.[1, 18, 23, 24]The RobustPrune Subroutine and Geometric DiversityBecause random directed graphs have poor local clustering and high routing diameters, Vamana iteratively restructures the graph's connections using a pruning mechanism.[1, 5, 18, 25] A major challenge in graph-based nearest neighbor search is the trade-off between graph sparsity and navigation efficiency.[5, 25, 26] A relative neighborhood graph constructs edges such that a new edge is only added if it is closer to the target point than to any existing neighbor.[25, 27] However, computing a relative neighborhood graph is computationally expensive (O(n^2)) and can produce pathologically long routing paths, such as near-line graphs with worst-case traversal costs of O(n).[1, 5, 25]To address this, Vamana utilizes the RobustPrune subroutine.[1, 5, 11] Given a target node p, a candidate neighbor set V_p (retrieved via graph traversal), a maximum out-degree constraint R, and an aggressiveness threshold parameter \alpha \ge 1.0, the procedure selectively prunes redundant edges [1, 20]:The candidates in V_p are sorted in ascending order based on their Euclidean distance to the target node p, where d(p, q) = |x_p - x_q|2.[1, 7, 11]The algorithm selects the closest remaining candidate p^* \in V_p and establishes a directed out-edge from p to p^ than to the target point p by a factor of \alpha is removed from the candidate set [1, 24]:\alpha \cdot d(p^*, p') \le d(p, p')This selection and pruning cycle repeats until the out-degree reaches the constraint limit R or the candidate pool is exhausted.[1, 11]The threshold parameter \alpha \ge 1.0 acts as a structural regulator.[20, 24] When \alpha = 1.0, the pruning logic matches the strict relative neighborhood graph formulation, removing points that are closer to an existing neighbor than to the target.[5, 23, 25] By scaling \alpha > 1.0, the system relaxes the pruning constraint.[1, 24] This relaxation allows slightly more distant points to be preserved as neighbors, provided they offer distinct directional shortcuts across the vector space.[1, 11, 24] This geometric diversity guarantees "multiplicative progress" at each step of a greedy search.[1] Consequently, the worst-case hop count of the graph drops from O(n) to O(\log n), ensuring efficient global routing properties.[1, 5, 23]Two-Pass Construction and Multi-Scale Edge RefinementTo optimize graph quality and construction speed, Vamana builds the graph in two sequential passes over the dataset.[1, 5, 23, 28] During both passes, the algorithm processes each vector p \in P in a randomized sequence.[1] For each point p, the system initiates a greedy graph search starting at the medoid s to locate the top L candidates closest to p, accumulating all visited nodes along the search path into a candidate pool V_p.[1, 5] It then executes the RobustPrune routine on V_p with the current pass-specific \alpha to establish the outgoing edge list for p.[1] Finally, reverse edges are added from the newly assigned neighbors back to p, and if any node's out-degree exceeds R, RobustPrune is re-run on its expanded edge list.[1, 20, 21]The two passes use different values for the threshold parameter \alpha to achieve multi-scale edge refinement [1, 5, 23]:First Pass (\alpha = 1.0): The first pass is built with the pruning threshold set to 1.0.[1, 5, 23, 29] This pass quickly smooths out the initial random regular graph, establishing short-range connections that align with the local manifold of the dataset.[5, 23]Second Pass (\alpha = \alpha{\text{user}} \ge 1.2): The second pass uses a higher user-configured pruning threshold, typically set between 1.2 and 2.0.[1, 5, 23, 29] Operating on the local pathways established in the first pass, this second pass selectively retains longer-range shortcuts.[5, 23] This multi-pass strategy decouples local clustering from global navigation, allowing the graph to support both high-velocity global routing and fine-grained local convergence.[1, 5, 11, 23]Tunable Alpha and Graph DiameterThe reachability parameter \alpha is a primary lever for controlling the graph's physical properties.[1, 24] Under the \alpha-reachability framework, the parameter governs a strict trade-off between index construction time, query traversal speed, and recall accuracy.[24] For any metric space (X, D), a graph G = (P, E) is said to be \alpha-reachable if, for any pair of vertices p, q \in P, there exists a path that guarantees multiplicative progress toward q at each step.[24] The pruning logic enforces that if p and q are not directly adjacent, there must exist an intermediate out-neighbor p' of p such that [24]:D(p', q) \cdot \alpha \le D(p, q)The theoretical implications of this reachability model are detailed in the Sorted Reachability theorem (Theorem 3.2), which mathematically defines the composite reachability of a graph pruned across multiple sequential stages.[24] For a graph G' constructed by merging or sequentially pruning with two distinct thresholds \alpha_1 and \alpha_2, the overall reachability in a general metric space is bounded by [24]:\text{Reachability}(G') \ge \frac{1}{\frac{1}{\alpha_1} + \frac{1}{\alpha_2}}When analyzed in a high-dimensional Euclidean space \mathbb{R}^d, the geometric constraints allow for a tighter bound that exploits spatial angles [24]:\text{Reachability}(G') \ge \frac{1}{\frac{1}{\alpha_1}\sqrt{1 - \frac{1}{4\alpha_2^2}} + \frac{1}{\alpha_2}\sqrt{1 - \frac{1}{4\alpha_1^2}}}This reachability guarantee ensures that greedy search paths cannot get trapped in local minima, allowing the routing algorithm to converge in logarithmically many steps O(\log n).[1, 5] By tuning \alpha dynamically, engineers can trade off a larger index size and longer construction time for reduced query latency and fewer physical disk reads.[23, 24]
Storage StrategyRAM: Compressed Product Quantization (PQ) CodesTo achieve a low memory footprint, DiskANN keeps uncompressed vectors out of RAM.[1, 8, 9] Instead, the system stores a compressed representation of the vectors in main memory.[1, 9] Product quantization (PQ) serves as the primary memory-saving technique.[1, 17, 27, 30]Under this scheme, the high-dimensional vector space \mathbb{R}^d is split into m orthogonal, lower-dimensional subspaces of dimension d/m.[9, 29, 31, 32] The sub-vectors in each subspace are clustered using K-means to generate a codebook of centroids.[9, 29, 31, 32] Each high-dimensional vector is then projected onto these subspaces, mapped to its nearest subspace centroids, and represented as a compact array of m codebook indices, each typically 1 byte in size.[1, 30, 32]During search operations, these RAM-resident product quantization codes are used to estimate distances between the query vector and candidate nodes.[1, 17, 30] By using look-up tables generated at query time, the system performs approximate distance calculations in main memory, completely avoiding disk reads during the initial candidate ranking phase.[17, 32, 33] Modern implementations optimize this pipeline by using Single Instruction, Multiple Data (SIMD) instructions.[32] For example, centroid-parallel SIMD computation pipelines stream codebook lookups directly through vector registers, keeping the active codebook resident in the processor's cache and reducing memory bus traffic.[32]SSD: Graph + Full-Precision VectorsThe structural Vamana graph index and the full-precision, uncompressed raw vectors are stored on an SSD.[1, 7, 34] To minimize random-access overhead and maximize solid-state drive throughput, DiskANN organizes this data using a highly structured disk layout.[1, 9, 29, 31] The raw vector data and the neighbor ID list for each point are stored together in a single, contiguous physical record on disk.[1, 9, 29, 31]Because the maximum out-degree of the graph is capped at R, each node's adjacency list is stored as a fixed-size array of R unsigned 32-bit integers, padded with zeros if the actual degree is less than R.[1, 19] This uniform sizing allows the system to calculate the exact disk offset of any node's record directly from its integer identifier without needing a memory-resident offset index.[1, 23]To prevent physical page fragmentation, DiskANN aligns these records with the SSD's sector boundaries, which are typically 4096 bytes on modern NVMe drives.[20, 31] Each sector contains an integer number of records, calculated as [31]:\text{Records per Sector} = \left\lfloor \frac{4096}{\text{Size of Vector} + \text{Size of Adjacency List}} \right\rfloorThis alignment prevents single records from spanning physical sector boundaries, ensuring that each disk access retrieves a complete, clean record.[31]This physical co-location creates a strong coupling between the graph's degree limit R, the vector dimension d, and the physical sector size.[1, 31] If R is configured too high, the size of a single record can exceed 4096 bytes.[31, 35] This forces the record to span multiple physical sectors, which triggers sector splitting and write/read amplification that can degrade query throughput.[31, 35] Consequently, the maximum graph degree must be budgeted alongside vector dimensionality to maintain high physical page utilization.[1, 31, 35]This out-of-core storage model allows a workstation with only 64 gigabytes of RAM to index and search billion-point datasets, such as SIFT1B, on a single commodity SSD.[7, 8, 9, 10] Purely in-memory graph methods like HNSW require massive distributed memory pools to host uncompressed datasets of this scale, making DiskANN a highly cost-effective alternative that achieves comparable query performance on a fraction of the hardware.[1, 7, 8, 36]64GB RAM for Billion-Point ScaleTo evaluate this out-of-core architecture, we analyze benchmarks on the billion-point SIFT1B and DEEP1B datasets.[7, 8, 9, 31, 36] These benchmarks demonstrate how DiskANN satisfies the three primary requirements of large-scale approximate search: high recall, low latency, and high density.[7, 8, 9]On a standard physical workstation configured with a 16-core CPU, 64 gigabytes of RAM, and a commodity NVMe SSD, DiskANN delivers the following search performance [7, 8, 9, 10, 31]:Query Throughput: Over 5000 queries per second (QPS) under highly concurrent query loads.[7, 8, 9, 31, 36]Latency Profile: Mean query latency of under 3 milliseconds (or under 5 milliseconds in cold-cache scenarios).[7, 8, 9, 31, 36]Retrieval Accuracy: Consistently delivers over 95% 1-recall@1.[7, 8, 9, 31, 36]In contrast, alternative billion-point systems with a similar 64GB memory footprint, such as FAISS and IVFOADC+G+P, plateau at approximately 50% 1-recall@1.[7, 8, 10, 31] To achieve a comparable 95% recall using purely in-memory graph structures like HNSW, a database operator would need to distribute the index across multiple physical machines to accommodate the uncompressed vectors in RAM.[1, 7, 8, 36] This distributed approach requires a massive hardware budget, highlighting the efficiency and cost-effectiveness of DiskANN's out-of-core, single-node architecture.[3, 7, 8, 36, 38]
Search OptimizationsBeamSearch and Parallel SSD ReadsTo search the SSD-resident index, DiskANN adapts the standard greedy graph traversal into a parallelized BeamSearch algorithm.[1, 19, 23, 39] Rather than expanding only the single closest unvisited candidate at each step, BeamSearch maintains a candidate pool of maximum size L and processes the top W closest unvisited candidates in parallel, where W is the beam width.[1, 17, 19, 23, 30]At each step of the traversal, the algorithm retrieves the neighborhood lists and raw vectors of these W candidates from the SSD.[1, 17, 19, 39] By using asynchronous, non-blocking I/O interfaces like Linux libaio or io_uring, DiskANN issues these W disk reads concurrently.[15, 40, 41, 42] This parallel execution utilizes the internal parallelism and queue depth of NVMe SSDs, allowing the system to overlap physical storage latency with the CPU overhead of distance calculations on the host.[15, 17, 40]The traversal path of this search process progresses through two distinct physical phases, each with different resource requirements [17]:Approach Phase: Starting from the cached medoid, the search navigates through high-degree shortcut edges toward the query vector's general neighborhood.[17] In this phase, using a large beam width can reduce I/O utilization (U_{\text{io}}).[17] This utilization is defined as the ratio of effectively explored nodes to total retrieved nodes [17]:U_{\text{io}} \triangleq \frac{N_{\text{eff}}}{N_{\text{read}}} = \frac{N_{\text{eff}}}{N_{\text{eff}} + N_{\text{rbu}}}where N_{\text{eff}} is the number of retrieved nodes that are actually explored, and N_{\text{rbu}} represents "read-but-unexplored" nodes.[17] Because large beam widths during the approach phase increase N_{\text{rbu}} without improving convergence speed, they can waste valuable NVMe IOPS.[17] 2. Converge Phase: As the search enters the query's local neighborhood, it switches to query-dependent, short-range edges to locate the final nearest neighbors.[17, 43] In this phase, increasing W is critical to ensure high recall and locate the true nearest neighbors.[17]To optimize this traversal, modern variants of DiskANN, such as PipeANN and VeloANN, use dynamic beam width scaling and coroutine-based scheduling.[15, 17, 40] These optimizations dynamically scale W based on the search phase, maximizing NVMe queue utilization and minimizing CPU idle time during disk I/O stalls.[15, 17, 40]Caching: Entry Point HopsBecause all search operations start at the global medoid s, the first few hops of any query path represent massive hotspots that are highly likely to be accessed by every query.[17, 18, 44] Reading these initial nodes from disk for every query would create a severe bottleneck, limiting overall throughput.[6, 8, 17, 43]To eliminate this overhead, DiskANN preloads and caches the top layers of the graph in main memory.[1, 17, 39, 44] Specifically, the system pre-calculates and caches all graph nodes located within a fixed number of hops C (typically C = 3 \text{ to } 4) from the entry medoid.[1, 17, 29, 39] This cache layout is computed using Dijkstra’s Single-Source Shortest Path (SSSP) algorithm during the index warmup phase.[17]This caching strategy behaves as a spatial low-pass filter, absorbing the highly predictable initial hops of the search path in RAM.[17, 43] Because the initial routing phases are structurally constrained by the graph's top-level topology, caching these nodes eliminates disk I/O during the early stages of traversal.[6, 17, 43, 44] This reserves the SSD's parallel queue exclusively for the late-stage convergence phase, where query-dependent nodes must be dynamically retrieved and cache hit rates naturally drop to 4%-9%.[6, 17, 43]Implicit Reranking: Full-Vector VerificationAlthough product quantization enables efficient in-memory distance estimation, its lossy nature introduces precision loss.[1, 7, 8, 17] If the final query results were returned using only these approximate RAM-resident coordinates, retrieval accuracy would be severely limited.[1, 7, 8]To achieve high recall, DiskANN integrates an implicit reranking mechanism directly into its graph traversal pipeline.[1, 2, 45] As the BeamSearch processes nodes, it reads their sector-aligned physical records from the SSD.[1, 9, 19, 31] Because each record bundles the uncompressed, full-precision vector alongside the node's neighbor IDs, retrieving a node's neighbor list automatically loads its uncompressed vector into memory without requiring an additional disk read.[1, 17]When calculating distances to candidate nodes, the system bypasses the lossy PQ codes and computes exact Euclidean or Cosine similarity using these uncompressed, high-precision vectors.[1, 45] This exact calculation is performed implicitly during candidate expansion, rather than as a separate post-processing step.[1, 2] By combining neighborhood retrieval and vector verification into a single physical I/O operation, DiskANN achieves the recall accuracy of uncompressed graph indices while keeping RAM requirements incredibly low.[1, 2, 7, 8, 9]
Construction MethodsOne-Shot Index ConstructionWhen the raw vector dataset and the resulting index structure fit entirely within the host system's main memory, DiskANN can build the index in a single step.[23, 31, 34] Under this "one-shot" approach, the entire Vamana sequential random insertion and dual-pass RobustPrune pipeline runs globally in RAM.[1, 18, 23, 31] Once construction is complete, the final logical graph is serialized into the sector-aligned on-disk layout alongside the raw vectors.[1, 20, 23, 31] While one-shot construction produces a highly accurate global graph, its memory requirement scales linearly with the size of the uncompressed dataset, making it impractical for workstations with limited RAM when handling massive, billion-point scales.[1, 7, 8, 9, 31]During one-shot construction, DiskANN optimizes cache locality and reduces memory allocation overhead by utilizing a flat, contiguous memory layout rather than nested dynamic arrays (e.g., standard standard vector-of-vectors structures).[23] This layout minimizes pointer-chasing overhead and ensures high data density during graph generation.[23] The computational complexity of the index building phase scales as:\text{Build Time Complexity} = O(n \cdot R \cdot L_{\text{build}})where n is the dataset size, R is the maximum out-degree, and L_{\text{build}} is the construction search list size.[23]Overlapping Partition and MergeTo index datasets that exceed available system memory (such as building a billion-point index on a workstation with only 64 gigabytes of RAM), DiskANN utilizes a divide-and-conquer strategy.[1, 7, 9, 31] This method partitions the dataset, builds smaller indices in memory, and merges them into a unified on-disk index.[1, 7, 9, 31]The construction pipeline operates as follows:K-Means Clustering: The uncompressed dataset is partitioned into k distinct clusters (such as k=40 clusters) using K-means++.[1, 9, 29, 31]Overlapping Distribution: To ensure connectivity between partitions, each raw vector point p is allocated to its nearest i clusters, with i=2 typically providing sufficient overlap.[1, 9, 29, 31]Sub-graph Generation: For each partition, the system builds an independent Vamana graph index on its uncompressed vectors.[1, 9, 29, 31] Because each partition contains only a fraction of the total dataset, these sub-graphs are constructed quickly in main memory.[1, 9, 29, 31]Adjacency Consolidation & Union: Once all sub-graphs are built, the system merges them by taking the union of their edge lists [1]:E_{\text{merged}} = \bigcup_{j=1}^{k} E_jPruning & Alignment: Because taking the union of the sub-graphs can cause some nodes to exceed the degree limit R, the system runs a final global RobustPrune pass (using the user-defined \alpha) over the merged adjacency list.[1, 20, 31] This ensures that each node's degree conforms to R before the final sector-aligned index file is written to the SSD.[20, 31]The decision to assign each point to i=2 clusters is a critical structural requirement.[1, 9, 29, 31] If points were distributed strictly to their single nearest cluster center (i=1), the merged graph would form a disjointed set of isolated topological components corresponding to each partition.[1] During a greedy search, the routing path would be unable to cross these partition boundaries, leading to catastrophic routing failures and severe recall degradation.[1]By deliberately overlapping the partitions, the shared points act as topological bridges.[1] These shared points provide the necessary connectivity for greedy search paths to navigate across partition boundaries, allowing the merged index to match the retrieval accuracy of a single-shot index built over the entire dataset.[1, 7, 31]To complete the index construction, the system performs a global uniform sampling at a rate of 150,000 / n to construct a highly representative warmup query set.[31] This query set is executed during system initialization to warm up the SSSP node cache, ensuring high cache hit rates from the very first production queries.[1, 29, 31]
Systems Integration and Operational RealitiesRelational Database Implementations (PostgreSQL)As vector databases transition from standalone niche engines to integrated enterprise transactional platforms, the out-of-core principles pioneered by DiskANN are being adopted by core database providers.[16, 35, 46, 47] Within the PostgreSQL ecosystem, two primary implementations of DiskANN have emerged to address the memory limitations of standard HNSW indexes:pgvectorscale: An open-source, self-hosted extension developed by Timescale that integrates StreamingDiskANN alongside pgvector.[4, 16, 35, 48] It builds upon pgvector's baseline types, allowing developers to scale database tables to tens of millions of vectors using local NVMe storage with bounded RAM footprints.[4, 16, 35]pg_diskann: A managed database extension developed by Microsoft Research and natively integrated into Azure Cosmos DB and Azure Database for PostgreSQL.[35, 45, 46, 49] It exposes a unified index type optimized for high concurrent query performance in cloud environments.[35, 45, 49]These implementations highlight the massive spatial density benefits of out-of-core architectures compared to in-memory indexes.[35] On a standardized production table containing 25,000 documents embedded with OpenAI's high-dimensional text-embedding-3-large model (3,072 dimensions), the physical storage footprints and build times of HNSW and DiskANN scale as follows [35]:The 21 megabyte physical size of pgvectorscale's DiskANN index represents a massive reduction in index overhead compared to pgvector's HNSW index.[35] This structural efficiency is critical when scaling to tens of millions of high-dimensional vectors, where purely memory-resident structures would require expensive, vertically scaled database hardware.[16, 35, 47]Dynamic Updates: Real-Time Inserts and DeletesWhile standard DiskANN is highly optimized for static datasets, many production databases require continuous real-time data ingestion and updates.[50, 51, 52, 53] Under traditional out-of-core graph indices, modifying the topological structures directly on disk is highly inefficient due to the severe write amplification of random small-batch writes.[52, 53]To address these limitations, dynamic indexing architectures like FreshDiskANN introduce specialized streaming update protocols.[50, 51, 53] FreshDiskANN supports real-time inserts and deletes while preserving stable search recall over time.[50, 51] Rather than buffering insertions globally in memory and batch-merging them to disk (which causes heavy write spikes and latency jitter), the storage layer utilizes a direct-insert model.[52]Under this model, inserting a new vector involves traversing the active on-disk graph to locate its nearest spatial neighbors.[52] The system then updates these neighboring records directly on the SSD, establishing backward and forward connections.[52] To mitigate write overhead and avoid complex garbage collection (GC) cycles, the index allocates extra space within each on-disk sector, reserving empty record slots to combine local updates in place.[52]Deletions are handled by applying a logical "tombstone" filter to the node.[50, 52] During graph traversal, the BeamSearch engine treats tombstoned vertices as transparent routing shortcuts—traversing through them to reach adjacent active candidates—while excluding them from final query results.[50, 54]This direct-insert and lazy-delete strategy achieves quiescent consistency, allowing systems to support thousands of concurrent updates per second without compromising query recall or latency.[50, 51, 52]

Source: DiskANN: Fast Accurate Billion-point Nearest Neighbor Search on a Single Node
URL: https://papers.nips.cc/paper/9527-rand-nsg-fast-accurate-billion-point-nearest-neighbor-search-on-a-single-node
DiskANN: Fast Accurate Billion-point Nearest Neighbor Search on a Single Node NeurIPS Proceedings Search DiskANN: Fast Accurate Billion-point Nearest Neighbor Search on a Single Node Suhas Jayaram Subramanya, Fnu Devvrit, Harsha Vardhan Simhadri, Ravishankar Krishnawamy, Rohan Kadekodi Advances in Neural Information Processing Systems 32 (NeurIPS 2019) AuthorFeedback Bibtex MetaReview Metadata Paper Reviews Abstract Current state-of-the-art approximate nearest neighbor search (ANNS) algorithms generate indices that must be stored in main memory for fast high-recall search. This makes them expensive and limits the size of the dataset. We present a new graph-based indexing and search system called DiskANN that can index, store, and search a billion point database on a single workstation with just 64GB RAM and an inexpensive solid-state drive (SSD). Contrary to current wisdom, we demonstrate that the SSD-based indices built by DiskANN can meet all three desiderata for large-scale ANNS: high-recall, low query latency and high density (points indexed per node). On the billion point SIFT1B bigann dataset, DiskANN serves > 5000 queries a second with < 3ms mean latency and 95%+ 1-recall@1 on a 16 core machine, where state-of-the-art billion-point ANNS algorithms with similar memory footprint like FAISS and IVFOADC+G+P plateau at around 50% 1-recall@1. Alternately, in the high recall regime, DiskANN can index and serve 5 − 10x more points per node compared to state-of-the-art graph- based methods such as HNSW and NSG. Finally, as part of our overall DiskANN system, we introduce Vamana, a new graph-based ANNS index that is more versatile than the graph indices even for in-memory indices. Source: DiskANN: Vector Search at Web Scale - Microsoft Research
URL: https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/research/project/project-akupara-approximate-nearest-neighbor-search-for-large-scale-semantic-search/
DiskANN: Vector Search at Web Scale - Microsoft Research Skip to main content Microsoft Security Azure Dynamics 365 Microsoft 365 Microsoft Teams Windows 365 Tech & innovation Microsoft AI Azure Space Mixed reality Microsoft HoloLens Microsoft Viva Quantum computing Sustainability Industries Education Automotive Financial services Government Healthcare Manufacturing Retail Partners Find a partner Become a partner Partner Network Microsoft Marketplace Software companies Resources Blog Microsoft Advertising Developer Center Documentation Events Licensing Microsoft Learn Microsoft Research View Sitemap Search Research Research Home Our research Resources Publications Code & data People Microsoft Research blog Research areas: Intelligence Artificial intelligence Audio & acoustics Computer vision Graphics & multimedia Human-computer interaction Human language technologies Search & information retrieval Research areas: Systems Data platforms and analytics Hardware & devices Programming languages & software engineering Quantum computing Security, privacy & cryptography Systems & networking Research areas: Theory Algorithms Mathematics Research areas: Other Sciences Ecology & environment Economics Medical, health & genomics Social sciences Technology for emerging markets Programs & events Academic programs Events & academic conferences Microsoft Research Forum Connect & learn Behind the Tech podcast Microsoft Research blog Microsoft Research Forum Microsoft Research podcast About People & news About Microsoft Research Careers & internships People Emeritus program News & awards Microsoft Research newsletter Microsoft Research Labs Africa AI for Science AI Frontiers Asia-Pacific Cambridge Health Futures India Montreal New England New York City Redmond Other labs Applied Sciences Mixed Reality & AI - Cambridge Mixed Reality & AI - Zurich Register: Research Forum DiskANN: Vector Search for All Overview People Publications Tools Groups Deep Learning-based embeddings are used widely for “dense retrieval” in information retrieval, computer vision, NLP, amongst others, owing to capture diverse types of semantic information. This paradigm constructs embeddings so that semantically similar items are closer in a high dimensional metric space. The first step to enabling search and recommendation with such embeddings is to index the embeddings of the corpus and support approximate nearest-neighbor search (ANNS) a.k.a. Vector Search for query embeddings. While ANNS is a fundamental problem has been studied for decades, existing algorithms suffer from two main drawbacks: either their search accuracies are low, thereby affecting the quality of results downstream, or their memory (DRAM) footprint is enormous, making it hard to serve them at web scale. In this project, we are designing algorithms to address the challenges of scaling ANNS for web and enterprise search and recommendation systems. Our goal is to build systems that serve trillions of points in a streaming setting cost effectively. Below is a summary of the associated research directions: DiskANN: (opens in new tab) an ANNS algorithm which can achieve both high accuracy as well as low DRAM footprint, by suitably using auxilliary SSD storage, which is significantly more cost-effective than DRAM. Using DiskANN, we can index 5-10X more points per machine than the state-of-the-art DRAM-based solutions: e.g., DiskANN can index upto a billion vectors while achieving 95% search accuracy with 5ms latencies, while existing DRAM-based algorithms peak at 100-200M points for similar latency and accuracy. FreshDiskANN (opens in new tab): We present the first graph-based ANNS index that reflects corpus updates into the index in real-time without compromising on search performance. Using the new graph update rules, we design a fresh-ANNS system that can index over a billion points on a workstation with an SSD and limited memory, and support thousands of concurrent real-time inserts, deletes and searches per second each, while retaining >95% 5-recall@5. Filtered Search (opens in new tab): We are also working on ANNS algorithms which can support vector search with auxiliary filters based on structured information. For example, retrieving the closest vectors which satisfy a certain criteria, such as date range, or region, or stock availability, etc. See the papers here (opens in new tab) and here (opens in new tab). Diverse Search: We are working on adding novel notions of diversity to our retrieval pipeline, to power applications like RAG (retrieval augmented generation). See papers here (opens in new tab) and here (opens in new tab). Out-of-distribution queries (opens in new tab): Data-dependent ANNS algorithms overfit to index dat distribution and are inaccurate for queries drawn from a distribution different than the index distribution. This is often the case in multi-modal datasets. This work addresses how to use a sample query set to improve the accuracy for such queries. Theoretical Understanding (opens in new tab): While graph-based ANNS has seen tremendous success in real-world applications, we don't understand when and how they work so effectively. One of our ongoing efforts is in obtaining a deeper understanding theoretically. The codebase is maintained at https://github.com/Microsoft/DiskANN. (opens in new tab) See also the NeurIPS'21 challenge (opens in new tab) on billion-scale ANNS algorithms, and report (opens in new tab) on winning ideas. Source: DistributedANN: Efficient Scaling of a Single DiskANN Graph Across Thousands of Computers - arXiv
URL: https://arxiv.org/html/2509.06046v1
DistributedANN: Efficient Scaling of a Single DiskANN Graph Across Thousands of Computers Back to arXiv
Back to arXiv This is experimental HTML to improve accessibility. We invite you to report rendering errors. Use Alt+Y to toggle on accessible reporting links and Alt+Shift+Y to toggle off. Report Issue Back to Abstract Download PDF Table of Contents Abstract 1 Introduction 2 DistributedANN 2.1 DiskANN Index Layout 2.2 Index Layout Modifications Compressed Vectors Duplicated into Graph Nodes. In-Memory Head index. 2.3 Near-data Computation 2.4 Orchestration Service 3 Constructing a Large Graph Index 4 Evaluation 4.1 Scaling 4.2 Reliability 4.3 Comparison with GPU-based systems 4.4 Comparison with Advanced Partitioning Schemes 5 Conclusion 5.1 Future Directions 6 Acknowledgments References License: CC BY-NC-SA 4.0 arXiv:2509.06046v1 [cs.DC] 07 Sep 2025 DistributedANN: Efficient Scaling of a Single DiskANN Graph Across Thousands of Computers Report issue for preceding element Philip Adams Menghao Li Shi Zhang Li Tan Qi Chen Mingqin Li Zengzhong Li Knut Risvik Harsha Vardhan Simhadri Report issue for preceding element Abstract Report issue for preceding element We present DistributedANN, a distributed vector search service that makes it possible to search over a single 50 billion vector graph index spread across over a thousand machines that offers 26 26 ms median query latency and processes over 100,000 queries per second. This is 6 × 6\times more efficient than existing partitioning and routing strategies that route the vector query to a subset of partitions in a scale out vector search system. DistributedANN is built using two well-understood components: a distributed key-value store and an in-memory ANN index. DistributedANN has replaced conventional scale-out architectures for serving the Bing search engine, and we share our experience from making this transition. Report issue for preceding element Information Retrieval, Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search, Distributed Systems, Search Engine Scalability 1 Introduction Report issue for preceding element Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) search is a common retrieval technique in web search, multimedia search, and new scenarios like Retrieval-Augmented Generation (Lewis et al., 2020) . Given a set of data vectors X X , and a query vector q q , the goal of an ANN search system is to quickly find as many of the k k vectors in X X that are nearest to q q as possible. ANN search is a classic problem and researchers have developed many techniques, including algorithmic approaches (Malkov & Yashunin, 2018; Andoni & Indyk, 2008; Subramanya et al., 2019; Wang., 2018; Babenko & Lempitsky, 2014) , compression techniques (Jegou et al., 2010; Ge et al., 2014; Gao & Long, 2024) , and specialized indexes targeting GPUs, flash storage, or external memory (Johnson et al., 2019; Ootomo et al., 2024; Wang, 2021; Jang et al., 2023) . The performance of these techniques is closely tracked by benchmarking efforts like ANN-Benchmarks (Aumüller et al., 2018) or Big-ANN-Benchmarks (Big-ANN Benchmarks, 2023) . Report issue for preceding element However, the majority of research effort has been focused on datasets small enough to fit in a single machine's memory or disk. For larger datasets, like searching over hundreds of billions of web documents, the standard approach is to split the corpus into smaller partitions 1 1 1 For online systems, it is often necessary to choose a partition size much smaller than the total space available on a machine, in order to be able to bring a new replica online quickly enough should a host fail.. An independent index is built for each partition, and each partition is hosted on a different machine so that the entire corpus can be searched in parallel. The main downside of this approach is efficiency: inside a single ANN index, query cost scales with log  | X | \log\lvert X\rvert (as empirically measured), while across many partitions (assuming a fixed partition size) the cost will scale with the number of partitions. In other words, a system with P partitions has a search complexity of P  log  | X | P P\log\frac{\lvert X\rvert}{P} , which is much worse than the complexity of one index over the entire dataset X X . Even techniques aimed at improving this tradeoff, like using clustering to assign vectors to partitions (Wang, 2021) , face scalability challenges and complex tradeoffs. Report issue for preceding element Clustered Partitioning DistributedANN Select best N partitions Cache Cache Cache Graph partition ( M SSD IO) Graph partition ( M SSD IO) Graph partition ( M SSD IO) … \ldots Orchestration Service Head Index Search Global Graph nodes ( BW SSD IO) 𝐇 \mathbf{H} graph hop batches Report issue for preceding element Figure 1: High-level architecture comparison between a conventional system using clustered partitioning and DistributedANN. Report issue for preceding element In this paper, we argue that state-of-the-art performance on very-large-scale datasets can be achieved through a single large logical index stored in a distributed key-value store. DistributedANN begins with the abstraction of the key-value store as a large shared disk, and then makes modifications to the data and compute placement choices of DiskANN indices in order to make it practical to serve them in this setting. We explore the tradeoffs compared to a traditional approach, and present experimental results demonstrating the scalability of this approach on a fifty-billion vector subset of a web search dataset. DistributedANN has already been deployed in Microsoft Bing to support search over hundreds of billions of vectors. Compared to the previous production system, it delivered over 6 × \times headroom in query throughput with the same machine footprint, and 7.8 and 4.5 percentage point improvements in recall@5 and recall@200 respectively. Report issue for preceding element 2 DistributedANN Report issue for preceding element Existing ANN indices like DiskANN are optimized for tiered storage by reducing the number of round-trips to SSD. Therefore, DiskANN is well suited for adaptation to a distributed serving environment, since the main drawback of accessing a networked disk is added access latency. However, directly serving a multi-hundred-terabyte DiskANN graph from network-attached storage encounters new bottlenecks: Report issue for preceding element • The memory-resident portions of the index are too large to fit in a single machine, and accessing them over the network would incur significant overhead. Report issue for preceding element • As the diameter of the graph grows, more hops are needed to traverse it, increasing latency. Report issue for preceding element • Transmitting the full graph node data over the network requires too much serial compute per query and consumes excessive network bandwidth. Report issue for preceding element In the rest of this section, we first review the data layout of DiskANN, and then discuss how we adapt the index to overcome these scaling bottlenecks. Report issue for preceding element 2.1 DiskANN Index Layout Report issue for preceding element A DiskANN index has 3 components: Report issue for preceding element An array of compressed (PQ or OPQ 2 2 2 Product Quantization and Optimized Product Quantization, described in detail in (Jegou et al., 2010; Ge et al., 2014) .) representations of all vectors in the index, stored in memory. Report issue for preceding element An array of graph nodes, one per vector, stored on SSD. Each node stores the full precision vector and a list of IDs representing out-neighbors. Report issue for preceding element An in-memory cache of frequently-accessed graph nodes. Report issue for preceding element Searching this index begins at a fixed point, and scores neighbor candidates of each visited node using their in-memory compressed representations to determine which nodes to visit in the next iteration of beam search. Depending on the index size and the recall required, tens to hundreds of nodes may be read (depending on I I , the limit on the total amount of IO), and for each node many candidate neighbors (depending on R R , the graph degree) will be considered. Report issue for preceding element 2.2 Index Layout Modifications Report issue for preceding element Compressed Vectors Duplicated into Graph Nodes. Report issue for preceding element Our first modification is based on the observation that, for a sufficiently large index, the array of compressed vectors will not be able to fit in a single machine. One option would be to store these compressed vectors in a memory-based key-value store. However, due to the large number of candidates that must be considered ( I × R I\times R may be tens of thousands of lookups per search for typical parameters) and the latency implications of doing an extra network hop per beam search iteration, we instead decide to duplicate the compressed representation of each vector into all the graph nodes it is a neighbor of. This introduces a significant space amplification 3 3 3 For parameters of R = 100 R=100 , d = 384 d=384 , d OPQ = 64 d_{\operatorname{OPQ}}=64 and using 8-byte IDs instead of 4-byte IDs to allow an index of more than 4 billion vectors, this is approximately a 10 10 x amplification. of Report issue for preceding element ( 1 + R )  sizeof  ( id ) + d + R  d OPQ R  sizeof  ( id ) + d , \frac{(1+R)\operatorname{sizeof}(\operatorname{id})+d+Rd_{\operatorname{OPQ}}}{R\operatorname{sizeof}(\operatorname{id})+d}, (1) but it reduces the number of read operations per search to I I . This approach mirrors that of (Tatsuno et al., 2024; Pan et al., 2023) . Report issue for preceding element In-Memory Head index. Report issue for preceding element We also observe that while a node cache in the key-value store still has the benefit of reducing IO operations, it does not provide the same latency benefit as in single-machine DiskANN because even cached nodes will still incur network hop latency when read. This is a major issue, since the shortest path to the furthest node from the starting point of the graph has at least log R  | X | \log_{R}\lvert X\rvert edges. In conventional DiskANN, the first few beam search iterations almost always hit the node cache, reducing the number of round trips to disk and thus the latency. To achieve a similar effect in DistributedANN, we introduce a dense cache of the top layers of the graph. We first conduct a breadth-first traversal to collect C C vectors from the top layers of the DiskANN graph. We then build a conventional sharded in-memory ANN index over these vectors. We call this smaller index the head index. At search time, we first search in the in-memory head index, and then use the results as the starting points for beam search of the DiskANN graph. Report issue for preceding element 2.3 Near-data Computation Report issue for preceding element Our next modification is also motivated by latency. While log scaling within a single index is favorable compared to linear scaling across indices, it will still require log  100 ≈ 6 \log 100\approx 6 times as much computation to achieve similar quality searching a 50-billion vector index as a 500-million vector one. If we treat the key-value store purely as a virtual disk and do this work sequentially in a single machine like in DiskANN, there will be a corresponding increase in latency. We instead introduce a near-data node scoring service running on each key-value host, described in Algorithm 1. Report issue for preceding element Algorithm 1 Node Scoring Service Input: Node keys { k i } {k_{i}} , threshold score t t , candidate limit l l , full-dimension query q q , SDC encoded query q SDC q_{\mathrm{SDC}} Static Data: OPQ distance table Output: Sorted result IDs and distances R R , sorted candidate IDs and distances C C Initialize R ← ∅ , C ← ∅ R\leftarrow\varnothing,C\leftarrow\varnothing Batch read the node entries n i n_{i} for all k i k_{i} for all n i n_{i} do Compute d  ( q , v ) d(q,v) for full-dimension vector v ∈ n i v\in n_{i} and insert v v into R R for all OPQ candidate p ∈ e i p\in e_{i} do if d OPQ  ( q SDC , p ) < t d_{\mathrm{OPQ}}(q_{\mathrm{SDC}},p)<t then Insert p p into C C end if end for end for Sort R R and partial-sort C C up to l l Truncate C C to l l Report issue for preceding element This change has the benefit of parallelizing the computation, while also allowing resources to be consumed in small uniform chunks (each key read will incur a similar amount of scoring work 4 4 4 We observe that due to the high ratio of shards to BW \operatorname{BW} , the typical batch size of this service is 1 1 , resulting in very predictable resource usage.) that work well with existing resource management/load balancing systems 5 5 5 Multiple scenarios search the web index, and each has a different ideal resource vs. quality tradeoff. By consuming resources in roughly equal chunks, the existing key-value store load balancer can transparently accommodate different search parameters. In a partitioned index, different search parameters required separate benchmarking to set appropriate load factors for each scenario.. Additionally, since we only transmit scores over the network instead of full nodes, we achieve a bandwidth savings 6 6 6 Using the same parameters as in Footnote 3, this is approximately a 6x saving. We increase the savings further by pruning any neighbors that are worse than the current worst member of the candidate heap before returning to the orchestration service. of Report issue for preceding element ( 1 + R )  ( sizeof  ( i  d ) + sizeof  ( s  c  o  r  e ) ) + d + d OPQ ( 1 + R )  sizeof  ( id ) + d + R  d OPQ \frac{(1+R)\left(\operatorname{sizeof}(id)+\operatorname{sizeof}(score)\right)+d+d_{\mathrm{OPQ}}}{(1+R)\operatorname{sizeof}(\operatorname{id})+d+Rd_{\operatorname{OPQ}}} (2) compared to a naive virtual disk approach. Report issue for preceding element 2.4 Orchestration Service Report issue for preceding element The final component of DistributedANN is an orchestration service that maintains lists of the best seen results and candidate vectors. This service will first issue a search in the head index, and then issue H H rounds of calls to the node scoring service before returning a final set of results to the caller, described formally in Algorithm 2. Report issue for preceding element Algorithm 2 Orchestration Service Input: Full dimension query vector q q . Beam width BW \mathrm{BW} . Beam iterations (hops) H H . Result count k k . Head index result count k head k_{\mathrm{head}} . Candidate size L ≥ max  ( BW , k ) L\geq\operatorname{max}(\mathrm{BW},k) . Static Data: OPQ distance table, OPQ codebooks Output: Sorted result IDs and distances R R Initialize result heap H R H_{R} of size k k , candidate heap H C H_{C} of size L L . Encode OPQ query q SDC q_{\mathrm{SDC}} using the codebooks. Search for k head k_{\mathrm{head}} results in the head index, and insert into H C H_{C} for i = 1 i=1 to H H do Let t = peekworst  ( H C ) t=\operatorname{peekworst}(H_{C}) Take best BW \mathrm{BW} candidates from H C H_{C} as keys K K . Let { R i } , { C i } = NodeScoring  ( K , t , L , q , q SDC ) {R_{i}},{C_{i}}=\operatorname{NodeScoring}(K,t,L,q,q_{\mathrm{SDC}}) . Partially merge-sorted-lists of { R i } {R_{i}} upto k k and { C i } {C_{i}} upto L L , then insert into respective heaps. end for Sort H R H_{R} into R R Report issue for preceding element Because this service has a small amount of persistent state, it can be hosted on many machines with low overhead, ensuring that the load is evenly distributed. This service is also able to use hedged requests (Dean & Barroso, 2013) , track replica health across requests, and allow partial failures of batches of node reads in order to reduce the tail latency normally associated with a high-fanout system like DistributedANN. Report issue for preceding element 3 Constructing a Large Graph Index Report issue for preceding element DiskANN graphs are typically built incrementally by searching with the vector to be inserted as a query, tracking all the visited nodes during the search for use as potential graph neighbors, and then pruning to at most R R actual neighbors. While it is possible to insert vectors into an DistributedANN graph by this procedure, it would require significantly more computation than building an equivalently sized partitioned graph, because the partitioned approach only needs to search in one smaller partition for each insertion. To reduce the graph construction cost, we employ a graph stitching approach similar to the one described in (Subramanya et al., 2019) . We first build an index with clustered partitioning, with vectors in the closure of multiple clusters inserted into all of them as described in (Wang, 2021) . Because these vectors occur in multiple partition's graphs, we are able to stitch together a unified graph by taking the union of their neighbors from all the partitions they are present in, as shown in Figure 2. In order to ensure that the entire graph is reachable, we build the head index from the union of the top layers of each partition's graph, rather than the top layers of the stitched-together graph. The quality of a graph built by this stitching process is lower than one built entirely incrementally, but is sufficient to get good results and is much faster to build. Report issue for preceding element Figure 2: A visual depiction of the unified graph construction process. Partitions are represented by colored regions, and neighborhoods by dark shading. When points exist in multiple partitions, their neighborhoods will be merged by taking the union of neighbor lists, yielding a unified graph. Report issue for preceding element 4 Evaluation Report issue for preceding element The Bing web index is composed of multiple independent slices to allow portions of the index to be updated atomically using less capacity than would be required to update the entire index atomically. Each slice consists of roughly 50 billion 384-dimensional int8 vectors. We compare the performance characteristics of DistributedANN and a traditional clustered partitioning approach (with roughly 200 million vectors per partition) on one slice of the index. Because of the graph stitching approach described in Section 3, we are able to ingest identical indexes for both approaches, though for DistributedANN we only ingest the first 72 neighbors in each node to reduce storage consumption. All experiments are conducted in a production environment which has a mix of host SKUs and multiple workloads sharing the resources of each host. A typical host in this environment has 256 to 768 GiB of memory, 5 to 10 TiB of SSD storage, roughly 200 IOPS per GiB of SSD storage, 32 to 64 physical cores, and 40 Gbps of network bandwidth. We ensure that the SKU mix hosting each system is roughly equivalent. The index parameters are chosen so that each system has a similar footprint (by bounding resource) at 15k QPS. The conventional index is bound by IO while DistributedANN is bound by SSD space and can continue scaling to over 100k QPS in the same footprint. Performance data was collected on a set of sampled web search queries, presented in Table 1. The parameters for DistributedANN are H = 5 , BW = 128 , R = 72 , k = L = 200 , k head = 200 H=5,\operatorname{BW}=128,R=72,k=L=200,k_{\mathrm{head}}=200 , with a head index size of 2.5 2.5 billion vectors. The index built with clustered partitioning selects the top 40 40 out of 203 203 partitions, and searches in each with parameters I = 120 , BW = 6 , R = 106 , k = L = 120 I=120,\operatorname{BW}=6,R=106,k=L=120 . Report issue for preceding element Table 1: Performance and accuracy comparison between DistributedANN and conventional approach with 3 replicas Report issue for preceding element 4.1 Scaling Report issue for preceding element Figure 3: IO in each cluster for a single query, as served by DistributedANN and a conventional clustered partitioning index. DistributedANN is able to implement a much more flexible traversal strategy, improving efficiency. Report issue for preceding element We observe that while DistributedANN does consume significantly more storage space and has higher latency than a conventional system, it uses significantly less IO and is able to achieve higher throughput and recall on the same graph. Because DistributedANN has unified graph, it can more efficiently allocate its IO budget, both by searching more deeply in relevant clusters and by touching more total clusters 7 7 7 Note that due to the graph stitching approach described in Section 3, a single read in DistributedANN may touch multiple clusters.. In comparison, a traditional clustered partitioning approach allocates a fixed amount of IO to each chosen partition (as shown in Figure 3). This allows DistributedANN to achieve consistently higher recall across a range of IO budgets, shown in Figure 4. Report issue for preceding element Figure 4: Optimal Recall/IO frontier for indexing 50 billion vectors. Grid search of parameters, DistributedANN: H H from 4 4 to 8 8 , BW = 32  i \operatorname{BW}=32i for i i from 3 3 to 6 6 . Clustered Partitioning: selected clusters N = { 20 , 25 , 30 , 40 , 50 , 60 } N=\left{20,25,30,40,50,60\right} , IO per cluster M = 32  i M=32i for i i from 2 2 to 6 6 . Report issue for preceding element DistributedANN also enjoys more flexible and efficient throughput scaling than a conventional system, because the underlying key-value store can be sharded across more hosts to increase the available IOPS and CPU. In a conventional architecture, increasing the number of partitions would require more partitions to be searched for each query, increasing the IO per query. So, the main way to improve throughput is by increasing the number of replicas, which requires resources to be allocated in much coarser-grained chunks and consumes additional SSD and memory in addition to the extra IOPS and CPU. Report issue for preceding element One significant bottleneck we identified in the current implementation of DistributedANN is the head index, which at 3 replicas becomes CPU-bound before the node scoring service. Our solution in the current system is to increase the number of head index replicas. Due to the relatively small size of the head index and the bounding resource of the overall system (SSD space), this does not increase the number of machines required to serve the index. Report issue for preceding element 4.2 Reliability Report issue for preceding element One potential concern for a distributed architecture is its resilience to issues in system components, like network partition or host failure. Although the mechanics of operating a high-availability distributed key-value store are well-understood in industry (DeCandia et al., 2007) , DistributedANN should still be resilient to partial failures in the node-storage layer. To test this resilience, we modified the node scoring service to accept a configurable failure rate parameter. We then examined the impact on recall@5 and recall@200 of different failure rates, shown in Table 2. Report issue for preceding element The service experiences a graceful degradation in recall roughly proportional to the failure rate. This not only gives confidence in the reliability of the system, but also the performance stability, as we can safely timeout node scoring requests experiencing tail latencies without a significant adverse effect on recall. Report issue for preceding element We contrast this with our experience operating a conventional partitioned ANN service. In such a service, availability is more difficult to maintain because of the higher partition sizes requiring more time to bring a new replica online. When a partition becomes unavailable, a large chunk of the dataset is missed, causing a dramatic drop in search quality. Additionally, when vectors are partitioned by clustering and only a subset of partitions are used for each search, load becomes imbalanced, as described in Subsection 4.4. The most popular clusters receive the most traffic and so are most likely to experience performance degradation, meaning that common queries will have the largest drop in search quality. In practice, independent partition scaling and heavy over-provisioning are required to achieve good availability, leading to lower resource utilization. Report issue for preceding element Table 2: Recall with degraded node scoring service availability Report issue for preceding element 4.3 Comparison with GPU-based systems Report issue for preceding element Recent work (Johnson et al., 2019; Zhao et al., 2020; Ootomo et al., 2024; Khan et al., 2024) has sought to exploit the massive parallelism of GPUs to greatly increase the search throughput of a single machine. These systems have meaningly shifted the tradeoff between vertical and horizontal scaling of ANN search, and offer extremely competitive price-to-performance for high-throughput, billion-scale indices. However, these systems still have limitations on the size of index that can fit in a single machine, and eventually encounter the same linear scaling properties and operational complexities as other partitioned indices on very large datasets. For our scenario, the scalability benefits of DistributedANN outweigh the advantages of GPU-based indices. Report issue for preceding element 4.4 Comparison with Advanced Partitioning Schemes Report issue for preceding element Recent work such as (Dong et al., 2019; Gottesbüren et al., 2024) improves the performance of partitioned graph indices through schemes that reduce the number of cross-partition edges in an k k -NN graph over the dataset. Further work is needed to compare the empirical performance of these approaches and DistributedANN. In particular, these approaches may be preferable in very latency-constrained scenarios where the multi-hop network overhead of DistributedANN is unacceptable. However, we feel that DistributedANN has some notable operational advantages: Report issue for preceding element • DistributedANN can use one algorithm to build the entire index, rather than separate approaches for coarse-grained partitioning and fine-grained index building. Because of the incremental insertion approach of DiskANN, build and search use the same code and can be optimized simultaneously. Report issue for preceding element • Semantic partitioning schemes are difficult to load-balance. Since queries will only access a subset of partitions, efficient serving requires independently scaling each partition with traffic, which is often difficult due to the different timescales between query pattern shifts and replica migration. By contrast, the underlying key-value store of DistributedANN is randomly sharded and so receives a predictable traffic distribution. Report issue for preceding element 5 Conclusion Report issue for preceding element DistributedANN achieves sublinear scaling of ANN search on very large datasets, in exchange for reasonable increases in latency and disk space overhead. It also reduces operational complexity by reusing existing distributed key-value store infrastructure. We feel these tradeoffs are favorable and now use DistributedANN in Microsoft Bing to enable search volume growth and quality improvements on the web index. Report issue for preceding element 5.1 Future Directions Report issue for preceding element We believe significant improvements in latency and space overhead are possible. Some opportunities include: Report issue for preceding element • DistributedANN uses traditional kernel-based TCP networking for remote service calls. Kernel-bypass networking would likely reduce the tail latency of these network calls significantly. More ambitiously, the relatively simple node scoring service logic could be implemented in a computational storage device attached to the orchestration host. Report issue for preceding element • As we note in Subsection 4.1, the head index quickly becomes compute bounded in our tests. It may be cost-effective to serve this index from GPU rather than increasing the number of replicas to serve more traffic. Report issue for preceding element • Because traditional DiskANN does not incur the space amplification that DistributedANN does from replicating compressed vectors into each node, techniques for reducing the average number of graph edges per vector have not been explored in depth. A significant reduction in space overhead is likely possible by placing multiple nearby full-dimension vectors into a single graph node, but further experimentation is needed to understand the tradeoffs of this approach. Report issue for preceding element • DistributedANN was designed to efficiently serve an index with relatively high traffic. However, further storage tiering to HDDs may allow efficient search on datasets with many mostly-cold tenants, such as per-user indices. Report issue for preceding element • DistributedANN is deployed across many machines in a cloud region. Inter-zone latency within a region can be up to 2 2 ms, and cross-rack bandwidth is oversubscribed. If DistributedANN were deployed on a dense cluster of machines with a fully connected network, performance would improve and it might become feasible to store the compressed vectors in a shared memory pool to reduce storage amplification. Report issue for preceding element 6 Acknowledgments Report issue for preceding element We would like to thank Dafan Liu, Gena Tertychnyi, and Adelin Miloslavov who gave valuable performance advice about the key-value store, node scoring service, and orchestration service. Report issue for preceding element Impact Statement Report issue for preceding element This paper presents work whose goal is to advance the field of Machine Learning. There are many potential societal consequences of our work, none which we feel must be specifically highlighted here. Open a report feedback form via keyboard, use " Ctrl + ?". Make a text selection and click the "Report Issue for Selection" button near your cursor. You can use Alt+Y to toggle on and Alt+Shift+Y to toggle off accessible reporting links at each section. Our team has already identified the following issues. We appreciate your time reviewing and reporting rendering errors we may not have found yet. Your efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because disability should not be a barrier to accessing research.
